other commentary : of caius julius caesar first publish in this edition 1915 reprint 1923 1929 introduction by thomas de quincey the character of the first caesar have perhaps never be worse appreciate than by he who in one sense describe it best that be with most force and eloquence wherever he really _ do _ comprehend it this be lucan who have nowhere exhibit more brilliant rhetoric nor wander more from the truth than in the contrast portrait of caesar and pompey the famous line _ nil actum reputan si quid superesset agendum _ be a fine feature of the real character finely express but if it have be lucan 's purpose -lrb- as possibly with a view to pompey 's benefit in some respects it be -rrb- utterly and extravagantly to falsify the character of the great dictator by no single trait could he more effectually have fulfil that purpose nor in fewer word than by this expressive passage _ gaudensque viam fecisse ruina _ such a trait would be almost extravagant apply even to marius who -lrb- though in many respects a perfect model of roman grandeur massy columnar imperturbable and more perhaps than any one man record in history capable of justify the bold illustration of that character in horace _ si fractus illabatur orbi impavidum ferient ruina _ -rrb- have however a ferocity in he character and a touch of the devil in he very rarely unite with the same tranquil intrepidity but for caesar the all-accomplished statesman the splendid orator the man of elegant habit and polished taste the patron of the fine art in a degree transcend all example of he own or the previous age and as a man of general literature so much beyond he contemporary except cicero that he look down even upon the brilliant syllum as a illiterate person -- to class such a man with the race of furious destroyer exult in the desolation they spread be to err not by a individual trait but by the whole genus the attila and the tamerlane who rejoice in avow themselves the scourge of god and the special instrument of he wrath have no one feature of affinity to the polished and humane caesar and would as little have comprehend he character as he could have respect theirs even cato the unworthy hero of lucan might have suggest to he a little more truth in this instance by a celebrated remark which he make on the characteristic distinction of caesar in comparison with other revolutionary disturber for say he whereas other have attempt the overthrow of the state in a continued paroxysm of fury and in a state of mind resemble the lunacy of intoxication caesar on the contrary among that whole class of civil disturber be the only one who have come to the task in a temper of sobriety and moderation _ -lrb- unum accessisse sobrium ad rempublicam delendam -rrb- _ great as caesar be by the benefit of he original nature there can be no doubt that he like other owe something to circumstance and perhaps amongst those which be most favourable to the premature development of great self-dependence we must reckon the early death of he father it be or it be not accord to the nature of man a advantage to be orphan at as early age perhaps utter orphanage be rarely or never such : but to lose a father betime may under appropriate circumstance profit a strong mind greatly to caesar it be a prodigious benefit that he lose he father when not much more than fifteen perhaps it be a advantage also to he father that he die thus early have he stay a year longer he might have see himself despise baffle and make ridiculous for where let we ask in any age be the father capable of adequately sustain that relation to the unique caius julius -- to he in the appropriate language of shakespeare the foremost man of all this world and in this fine and caesarean line this world be to be understand not of the order of co-existence merely ` but also of the order of succession he be the foremost man not only of he contemporary but also within he own intellectual class of man generally -- of all that ever should come after he or should sit on throne under the denomination of czar kesar or caesar of the bosphorus and the danube of all in every age that should inherit he supremacy of mind or should subject to themselves the generation of ordinary man by quality analogous to he of this infinite superiority some part must be ascribe to he early emancipation from paternal control there be very many case in which simply from consideration of sex a female can not stand forward as the head of a family or as its suitable representative if they be even lady paramount and in situation of command they be also woman the staff of authority do not annihilate they sex and scruple of female delicacy interfere for ever to unnerve and emasculate in they hand the sceptre however otherwise potent hence we see in noble family the merest boy put forward to represent the family dignity as fitter supporter of that burden than they mature mother and of caesar 's mother though little be record and that little incidentally this much at least we learn -- that if she look down upon he with maternal pride and delight she look up to he with female ambition as the re-edifier of she husband 's honor -- look with reverence as to a column of the roman grandeur and with fear and feminine anxiety as to one whose aspiring spirit carry he but too prematurely into the field of adventurous strife one slight and evanescent sketch of the relation which subsist between caesar and he mother catch from the wreck of time be preserve both by plutarch and suetonius we see in the early dawn the young patrician standing upon the step of he patrimonial portico he mother with she arm wreathe about he neck look up to he noble countenance sometimes draw augury of hope from feature so fit for command sometimes bode a early blight to promise so dangerously magnificent that she have something of she son 's aspiring character or that he presume so much in a mother of he we learn from the few word which survive of they conversation he address to she no language that could tranquillise she fear on the contrary to any but a roman mother he valedictory word take in connexion with the known determination of he character be of a nature to consummate she depression as they tend to confirm the very worst of she fear he be then go to stand he chance in a popular electioneering contest for a office of the highest dignity and to launch himself upon the storm of the campus martius at that period besides other and more ordinary danger the band of gladiator keep in the pay of the more ambitious or turbulent amongst the roman noble give a popular tone of ferocity and of personal risk to the course of such contest and either to forestall the victory of a antagonist or to avenge they own defeat it be not at all impossible that a body of incensed competitor might intercept he final triumph by assassination for this danger however he have no leisure in he thought of consolation the sole danger which _ he _ contemplated or suppose he mother to contemplate be the danger of defeat and for that he reserve he consolation he bid she fear nothing for that he determination be to return with victory and with the ensign of the dignity he seek or to return a corpse early indeed do caesar 's trial commence and it be probable that have not the death of he father by throw he prematurely upon he own resource prematurely develop the masculine feature of he character force he whilst yet a boy under the discipline of civil conflict and the yoke of practical life even _ he _ energy might have be insufficient to sustain they he age be not exactly ascertain but it be past a doubt that he have not reach he twentieth year when he have the hardihood to engage in a struggle with syllum then dictator and exercise the immoderate power of that office with the licence and the severity which history have make so memorable he have neither any distinct grounds of hope nor any eminent example at that time to countenance he in this struggle -- which yet he push on in the most uncompromising style and to the utmost verge of defiance the subject of the contest give it a further interest it be the youthful wife of the youthful caesar who stand under the shadow of the great dictator 's displeasure not personally but politically on account of she connexion : and she it be cornelia the daughter of a man who have be four time consul that caesar be require to divorce : but he spurn the haughty mandate and carry he determination to a triumphant issue notwithstanding he life be at stake and at one time save only by shift he place of concealment every night and this young lady it be who afterwards become the mother of he only daughter both mother and daughter it be remarkable perish prematurely and at critical period of caesar 's life for it be probable enough that these irreparable wound to caesar 's domestic affection throw he with more exclusiveness of devotion upon the fascination of glory and ambition than might have happen under a happier condition of he private life that caesar should have escape destruction in this unequal contest with a enemy then wield the whole thunder of the state be somewhat surprising and historian have seek they solution of the mystery in the powerful intercession of the vestal virgin and several other of high rank amongst the connexion of he great house these may have do something but it be due to syllum who have a sympathy with everything truly noble to suppose he strike with powerful admiration for the audacity of the young patrician stand out in such severe solitude among so many example of timid concession and that to this magnanimous feeling in the dictator much of the indulgence which he show may have be really due in fact accord to some account it be not syllum but the creature of syllum -lrb- _ adjutore _ -rrb- who pursue caesar we know at all event that sylla form a right estimate of caesar 's character and that from the complexion of he conduct in this one instance he draw that famous prophecy of he future destiny bidding he friend beware of that slipshod boy for that in he lay couchant many a marius a grander testimony to the awe which caesar inspire or from one who know better the quality of that cyclopean man by whose scale he measure the patrician boy can not be imagine it be not we intention or consistent with we plan to pursue this great man through the whole circumstance of he romantic career though it be certain that many part of he life require investigation much keener than have ever be apply to they and that many might be place in a new light indeed the whole of this most momentous section of ancient history ought to be recompose with the critical scepticism of a niebuhr and the same comprehensive collation rest if possible on the felicitous interpretation of authority in reality it be the hinge upon which turn the future destiny of the whole earth and have therefore a common relation to all modern nation whatsoever should naturally have be cultivate with the zeal which belong to a personal concern in general the anecdote which express most vividly the grandeur of character in the first caesar be those which illustrate he defiance of danger in extremity : the prodigious energy and rapidity of he decision and motion in the field -lrb- look to which it be that cicero call he -lrb- greek : tera -rrb- or portentous revelation -rrb- the skill with which he penetrate the design of he enemy and the electric speed with which he meet disaster with remedy and reparation or where that be impossible with relief the extraordinary presence of mind which he show in turn adverse omen to he own advantage as when upon stumble in come on shore -lrb- which be esteem a capital omen of evil -rrb- he transfigure as it be in one instant its whole meaning by exclaim thus and by this contact with the earth do i take possession of thee o africa in that way give to a accident the semblance of a symbolic purpose equally conspicuous be the grandeur of fortitude with which he face the whole extent of a calamity when palliation could do no good non negando minuendove sed insuper amplificando _ ementiendoque _ as when upon find he soldiery alarm at the approach of juba with force really great but exaggerated by they terror he address they in a military harangue to the following effect : -- know that within a few day the king will come up with we bring with he sixty thousand legionary thirty thousand cavalry one hundred thousand light troops besides three hundred elephant such be the case let I hear no more of conjecture and opinion for you have now my warrant for the fact whose information be past doubt therefore be satisfied otherwise i will put every man of you on board some crazy old fleet and whistle you down the tide -- no matter under what wind no matter towards what shore finally we might seek for _ characteristic _ anecdote of caesar in he unexampled liberality and contempt of money upon this last topic it be the just remark of casaubon that some instance of caesar 's munificence have be think apocryphal or to rest upon false reading simply from ignorance of the heroic scale upon which the roman splendor of that age proceed a forum which caesar build out of the product of he last campaign by way of a present to the roman people cost he -- for the ground merely on which it stand -- nearly eight hundred thousand pound to the citizen of rome he present in one _ congiary _ about two guinea and a half a head to he army in one _ donation _ upon the termination of the civil war he give a sum which allow about two hundred pound a man to the infantry and four hundred to the cavalry it be true that the legionary troops be then much reduce by the sword of the enemy and by the tremendous hardship of they last campaign in this however he do perhaps no more than repay a debt for it be a instance of military attachment beyond all that wallenstein or any commander the most beloved amongst he troops have ever experience that on the break out of the civil war not only do the centurion of every legion severally maintain a horse soldier but even the private volunteer to serve without pay and -lrb- what might seem impossible -rrb- without they daily ration this be accomplish by subscription amongst themselves the more opulent undertaking for the maintenance of the needy they disinterested love for caesar appear in another and more difficult illustration : it be a traditionary anecdote in rome that the majority of those amongst caesar 's troops who have the misfortune to fall into the enemy 's hand refuse to accept they life under the condition of serve against _ he _ in connexion with this subject of he extraordinary munificence there be one aspect of caesar 's life which have suffer much from the misrepresentation of historian and that be -- the vast pecuniary embarrassment under which he labor until the profit of war have turn the scale even more prodigiously in he favor at one time of he life when appoint to a foreign office so numerous and so clamorous be he creditor that he could not have leave rome on he public duty have not crassus come forward with assistance in money or by guarantee to the amount of nearly two hundred thousand pound and at another he be accustom to amuse himself with compute how much money it would require to make he worth exactly nothing -lrb- _ ie _ simply to clear he of debt -rrb- this by one account amount to upward of two million sterling now the error of historian have be to represent these debt as the original ground of he ambition and he revolutionary project as though the desperate condition of he private affair have suggest a civil war to he calculation as the best or only mode of redress it such a policy would have resemble the last desperate resource of a unprincipled gambler who on see he final game at chess and the accumulate stake depend upon it all on the brink of irretrievable sacrifice dexterously upset the chess-board or extinguish the light but julius the one sole patriot of rome could find no advantage to he plan in darkness or in confusion honestly support he would have crush the oligarchy of rome by crush in its lair that venal and hunger-bitten democracy which make oligarchy and its machinery resistless caesar 's debt far from be stimulant and exciting cause of he political ambition stand in a inverse relation to the ambition they be its result and represent its natural cost be contract from first to last in the service of he political intrigue for raise and maintain a powerful body of partisan both in rome and elsewhere whosoever indeed will take the trouble to investigate the progress of caesar 's ambition from such material as even yet remain may satisfy himself that the scheme of revolutionize the republic and place himself at its head be no growth of accident or circumstance above all that it do not arise upon any so petty and indirect a suggestion as that of he debt but that he debt be in they very first origin purely ministerial to he wise indispensable and patriotic ambition and that he revolutionary plan be at all period of he life a direct and foremost object but in no case bottom upon casual impulse in this there be not only patriotism but in fact the one sole mode of patriotism which could have prosper or could have find a field of action chatter not sublime reader commonplace of scoundrel moralist against ambition in some case ambition be a hopeful virtue in other -lrb- as in the rome of we resplendent julius -rrb- ambition be the virtue by which any other could flourish it have become evident to everybody that rome under its present constitution must fall and the sole question be -- by whom even pompey not by nature of a aspiring turn and prompt to he ambitious course undoubtedly by circumstance and the friend who besiege he be in the habit of say syllum potuit : ego non potero _ syllum find it possible : shall i find it not so _ possible to do what to overthrow the political system of the republic this have silently collapse into a order of thing so vicious grow also so hopelessly worse that all honest patriot invoke a purify revolution even though buy at the heavy price of a tyranny rather where no benefit of choice be allow to rome as respect the thing but only as respect the person caesar have the same right to enter the arena in the character of combatant as could belong to any one of he rival and that he _ do _ enter that arena constructively and by secret design from he very earliest manhood may be gather from this -- that he suffer no opening towards a revolution provide they have any hope in they to escape he participation it be familiarly know that he be engage pretty deeply in the conspiracy of catiline and that he incur considerable risk on that occasion but it be less would have be denounce to the senate such then for its purpose and its uniform motive be the sagacious munificence of caesar apart from this motive and consider in and for itself and simply with a blackie 's latin text 1905-7 \* \* \* \* \* contents the war in gaul the civil war the commentary of caius julius caesar the war in gaul book i i -- all gaul be divide into three part one of which the belgae inhabit the aquitani another those who in they own language be call celt in ours gaul the third all these differ from each other in language custom and law the river garonne separate the gaul from the aquitanus the marne and the seine separate they from the belga of all these the belga be the bravest because they be farthest from the civilisation and refinement of -lrb- we -rrb- province and merchant least frequently resort to they and import those thing which tend to effeminate the mind and they be the nearest to the german who dwell beyond the rhine with whom they be continually wage war for which reason the helvetius also surpass the rest of the gaul in valor as they contend with the german in almost daily battle when they either repel they from they own territory or themselves wage war on they frontier one part of these which it have be say that the gaul occupy take its beginning at the river rhone : it be bound by the river garonne the ocean and the territory of the belgae : it border too on the side of the sequanus and the helvetius upon the river rhine and stretch towards the north the belgae rise from the extreme frontier of gaul extend to the lower part of the river rhine and look towards the north and the rise sun aquitanium extend from the river garonne to the pyrenaean mountain and to that part of the ocean which be near spain : it look between the setting of the sun and the north star ii -- among the helvetii orgetorix be by far the most distinguished and wealthy he when marcus messalum and marcus piso be consul incite by lust of sovereignty form a conspiracy among the nobility and persuade the people to go forth from they territory with all they possession -lrb- say -rrb- that it would be very easy since they excel all in valor to acquire the supremacy of the whole of gaul to this he the more easily persuade they because the helvetius be confine on every side by the nature of they situation on one side by the rhine a very broad and deep river which separate the helvetian territory from the german on a second side by the jura a very high mountain which be -lrb- situate -rrb- between the sequanus and the helvetius on a third by the lake of geneva and by the river rhone which separate we province from the helvetius from these circumstance it result that they could range less widely and could less easily make war upon they neighbor for which reason man fond of war -lrb- as they be -rrb- be affect with great regret they think that consider the extent of they population and they renown for warfare and bravery they have but narrow limit although they extend in length 240 and in breadth 180 -lrb- roman -rrb- mile iii -- induce by these consideration and influence by the authority of orgetorix they determine to provide such thing as be necessary for they expedition -- to buy up as great a number as possible of beast of burden and waggon -- to make they sowing as large as possible so that on they march plenty of corn might be in store -- and to establish peace and friendship with the neighbouring state they reckon that a term of two year would be sufficient for they to execute they design they fix by decree they departure for the third year orgetorix be choose to complete these arrangement he take upon himself the office of ambassador to the state : on this journey he persuade casticus the son of catamantalede -lrb- one of the sequani whose father have possess the sovereignty among the people for many year and have be style _ friend _ by the senate of the roman people -rrb- to seize upon the sovereignty in he own state which he father have hold before he and he likewise persuade dumnorix a aeduan the brother of divitiacus who at that time possess the chief authority in the state and be exceedingly beloved by the people to attempt the same and give he he daughter in marriage he prove to they that to accomplish they attempt be a thing very easy to be do because he himself would obtain the government of he own state that there be no doubt that the helvetius be the most powerful of the whole of gaul he assure they that he will with he own force and he own army acquire the sovereignty for they incite by this speech they give a pledge and oath to one another and hope that when they have seize the sovereignty they will by means of the three most powerful and valiant nation be enable to obtain possession of the whole of gaul iv -- when this scheme be disclose to the helvetius by informer they accord to they custom compel orgetorix to plead he cause in chain it be the law that the penalty of be burn by fire should await he if condemn on the day appoint for the plead of he cause orgetorix draw together from all quarter to the court all he vassal to the number of ten thousand person and lead together to the same place and all he dependant and debtor-bondsman of whom he have a great number by means of these he rescue himself from -lrb- the necessity of -rrb- plead he cause while the state incense at this act be endeavor to assert its right by arm and the magistrate be muster a large body of man from the country orgetorix die and there be not want a suspicion as the helvetii think of he have commit suicide v -- after he death the helvetius nevertheless attempt to do that which they have resolve on namely to go forth from they territory when they think that they be at length prepare for this undertaking they set fire to all they town in number about twelve -- to they village about four hundred -- and to the private dwelling that remain they burn up all the corn except what they intend to carry with they that after destroy the hope of a return home they might be the more ready for undergo all danger they order every one to carry forth from home for himself provision for three month ready ground they persuade the rauracus and the tulingus and the latobrigus they neighbor to adopt the same plan and after burn down they town and village to set out with they : and they admit to they party and unite to themselves as confederate the boius who have dwell on the other side of the rhine and have cross over into the norican territory and assault noreia vi -- there be in all two route by which they could go forth from they country -- one through the sequani narrow and difficult between mount jura and the river rhone -lrb- by which scarcely one waggon at a time could be lead there be moreover a very high mountain overhang so that a very few might easily intercept they -rrb- the other through we province much easier and freer from obstacle because the rhone flow between the boundary of the helvetius and those of the allobroge who have lately be subdue and be in some place cross by a ford the furthest town of the allobroge and the nearest to the territory of the helvetius be geneva from this town a bridge extend to the helvetius they think that they should either persuade the allobroge because they do not seem as yet well-affected towards the roman people or compel they by force to allow they to pass through they territory have provide everything for the expedition they appoint a day on which they should all meet on the bank of the rhone this day be the fifth before the kalends of April -lrb- _ ie _ the 28th of march -rrb- in the consulship of lucius piso and aulus gabinius -lrb- bc 58 -rrb- vii -- when it be report to caesar that they be attempt to make they route through we province he hasten to set out from the city and by as great march as he can proceeds to further gaul and arrive at geneva he order the whole province -lrb- to furnish -rrb- as great a number of soldier as possible as there be in all only one legion in further gaul : he order the bridge at geneva to be break down when the helvetius be apprise of he arrival they send to he as ambassador the most illustrious man of they state -lrb- in which embassy numeius and verudoctius hold the chief place -rrb- to say that it be they intention to march through the province without do any harm because they have -lrb- accord to they own representation -rrb- no other route : -- that they request they might be allow to do so with he consent caesar inasmuch as he keep in remembrance that lucius cassius the consul have be slay and he army route and make to pass under the yoke by the helvetius do not think that -lrb- they request -rrb- ought to be grant nor be he of opinion that man of hostile disposition if a opportunity of march through the province be give they would abstain from outrage and mischief yet in order that a period might intervene until the soldier whom he have order -lrb- to be furnish -rrb- should assemble he reply to the ambassador that he would take time to deliberate if they want anything they might return on the day before the ides of April -lrb- on April 12th -rrb- viii -- meanwhile with the legion which he have with he and the soldier who have assemble from the province he carry along for nineteen -lrb- roman not quite eighteen english -rrb- mile a wall to the height of sixteen foot and a trench from the lake of geneva which flow into the river rhone to mount jura which separate the territory of the sequanus from those of the helvetius when that work be finish he distribute garrison and closely fortify redoubt in order that he may the more easily intercept they if they should attempt to cross over against he will when the day which he have appoint with the ambassador come and they return to he he say that he can not consistently with the custom and precedent of the roman people grant any one a passage through the province and he give they to understand that if they should attempt to use violence he would oppose they the helvetii disappointed in this hope try if they could force a passage -lrb- some by means of a bridge of boat and numerous raft construct for the purpose other by the ford of the rhone where the depth of the river be least sometimes by day but more frequently by night -rrb- but be keep at bay by the strength of we work and by the concourse of the soldier and by the missile they desist from this attempt ix -- there be leave one way -lrb- namely -rrb- through the sequanus by which on account of its narrowness they could not pass without the consent of the sequanus as they could not of themselves prevail on they they send ambassador to dumnorix the aeduan that through he intercession they might obtain they request from the sequani dumnorix by he popularity and liberality have great influence among the sequanus and be friendly to the helvetius because out of that state he have marry the daughter of orgetorix and incite by lust of sovereignty be anxious for a revolution and wish to have as many state as possible attach to he by he kindness towards they he therefore undertake the affair and prevail upon the sequanus to allow the helvetius to march through they territory and arrange that they should give hostage to each other -- the sequanus not to obstruct the helvetius in they march -- the helvetius to pass without mischief and outrage x -- it-is again tell caesar that the helvetii intend to march through the country of the sequanus and the aeduus into the territory of the santone which be not far distant from those boundary of the tolosate which -lrb- viz tolosa toulouse -rrb- be a state in the province if this take place he see that it would be attend with great danger to the province to have warlike man enemy of the roman people border upon a open and very fertile tract of country for these reason he appoint titus labienus he lieutenant to the command of the fortification which he have make he himself proceed to italy by force march and there levy two legion and lead out from winter-quarter three which be winter around aquileium and with these five legion march rapidly by the nearest route across the alp into further gaul here the centrone and the graiocelus and the caturige have take possession of the higher part attempt to obstruct the army in they march after have route these in several battle he arrive in the territory of the vocontius in the further province on the seventh day from ocelum which be the most remote town of the hither province thence he lead he army into the country of the allobroge and from the allobroge to the segusianus these people be the first beyond the province on the opposite side of the rhone xi -- the helvetius have by this time lead they force over through the narrow defile and the territory of the sequanus and have arrive at the territory of the aeduus and be ravage they land the aeduus as they could not defend themselves and they possession against they send ambassador to caesar to ask assistance -lrb- plead -rrb- that they have at all time so well deserve of the roman people that they field ought not to have be lay waste -- they child carry off into slavery -- they town storm almost within sight of we army at the same time the ambarrus the friend and kinsman of the aeduus apprise caesar that it be not easy for they now that they field have be devastate to ward off the violence of the enemy from they town : the allobroge likewise who have village and possession on the other side of the rhone betake themselves in flight to caesar and assure he that they have nothing remain except the soil of they land caesar induce by these circumstance decide that he ought not to wait until the helvetius after destroy all the property of he ally should arrive among the santone xii -- there be a river -lrb- call -rrb- the saone which flow through the territory of the aeduus and sequanus into the rhone with such incredible slowness that it can not be determine by the eye in which direction it flow this the helvetius be cross by raft and boat join together when caesar be inform by spy that the helvetius have already convey three part of they force across that river but that the fourth part be leave behind on this side of the saone he set out from the camp with three legion during the third watch and come up with that division which have not yet cross the river attack they encumber with baggage and not expect he he cut to piece a great part of they the rest betook themselves to flight and conceal themselves in the nearest wood that canton -lrb- which be cut down -rrb- be call the tigurine for the whole helvetian state be divide into four canton this single canton have leave they country within the recollection of we father have slay lucius cassius the consul and have make he army pass under the yoke -lrb- bc 107 -rrb- thus whether by chance or by the design of the immortal god that part of the helvetian state which have bring a signal calamity upon the roman people be the first to pay the penalty in this caesar avenge not only the public but also he own personal wrong because the tigurinus have slay lucius piso the lieutenant -lrb- of cassius -rrb- the grandfather of lucius calpurnius piso he -lrb- caesar 's -rrb- father-in-law in the same battle as cassius himself xiii -- this battle end that he might be able to come up with the remain force of the helvetius he procure a bridge to be make across the saone and thus lead he army over the helvetii confuse by he sudden arrival when they find that he have effect in one day what they themselves have with the utmost difficulty accomplish in twenty namely the cross of the river send ambassador to he at the head of which embassy be divico who have be commander of the helvetius in the war against cassius he thus treat with caesar : -- that if the roman people would make peace with the helvetius they would go to that part and there remain where caesar might appoint and desire they to be but if he should persist in persecute they with war that he ought to remember both the ancient disgrace of the roman people and the characteristic valor of the helvetius as to he have attack one canton by surprise -lrb- at a time -rrb- when those who have cross the river could not bring assistance to they friend that he ought not on that account to ascribe very much to he own valor or despise they that they have so learn from they sire and ancestor as to rely more on valor than on artifice or stratagem wherefore let he not bring it to pass that the place where they be stand should acquire a name from the disaster of the roman people and the destruction of they army or transmit the remembrance -lrb- of such a event to posterity -rrb- xiv -- to these word caesar thus reply : -- that on that very account he feel less hesitation because he keep in remembrance those circumstance which the helvetian ambassador have mention and that he feel the more indignant at they in proportion as they have happen undeservedly to the roman people : for if they have be conscious of have do any wrong it would not have be difficult to be on they guard but for that very reason have they be deceive because neither be they aware that any offence have be give by they on account of which they should be afraid nor do they think that they ought to be afraid without cause but even if he be willing to forget they former outrage could he also lay aside the remembrance of the late wrong in that they have against he will attempt a route through the province by force in that they have molest the aeduus the ambarrus and the allobroge that as to they so insolently boast of they victory and as to they be astonish that they have so long commit they outrage with impunity -lrb- both these thing -rrb- tend to the same point for the immortal god be wont to allow those person whom they wish to punish for they guilt sometimes a greater prosperity and longer impunity in order that they may suffer the more severely from a reverse of circumstance although these thing be so yet if hostage be to be give he by they in order that he may be assure they will do what they promise and provide they will give satisfaction to the aeduus for the outrage which they have commit against they and they ally and likewise to the allobroge he -lrb- caesar -rrb- will make peace with they divico reply that the helvetius have be so train by they ancestor that they be accustom to receive not to give hostage of that fact the roman people be witness have give this reply he withdraw xv -- on the follow day they move they camp from that place caesar do the same and send forward all he cavalry to the number of four thousand -lrb- which he have draw together from all part of the province and from the aeduus and they ally -rrb- to observe towards what part the enemy be direct they march these have too eagerly pursue the enemy 's rear come to a battle with the cavalry of the helvetius in a disadvantageous place and a few of we man fall the helvetii elated with this battle because they have with five hundred horse repulse so large a body of horse begin to face we more boldly sometimes too from they rear to provoke we man by a attack caesar -lrb- however -rrb- restrain he man from battle deem it sufficient for the present to prevent the enemy from rapine forage and depredation they march for about fifteen day in such a manner that there be not more than five or six mile between the enemy 's rear and we van xvi -- meanwhile caesar keep daily importune the aeduus for the corn which they have promise in the name of they state for in consequence of the coldness -lrb- gaul being as before say situate towards the north -rrb- not only be the corn in the field not ripe but there be not in store a sufficiently large quantity even of fodder : besides he be unable to use the corn which he have convey in ship up the river saone because the helvetius from whom he be unwilling to retire have divert they march from the saone the aeduus keep defer from day to day and say that it be be collect -- bring in -- on the road when he see that he be put off too long and that the day be close at hand on which he ought to serve out the corn to he soldier -- have call together they chief of whom he have a great number in he camp among they divitiacus and liscus who be invest with the chief magistracy -lrb- whom the aedui style the vergobretus and who be elect annually and have power of life and death over he countryman -rrb- he severely reprimand they because he be not assist by they on so urgent a occasion when the enemy be so close at hand and when -lrb- corn -rrb- could neither be buy nor take from the field particularly as in a great measure urge by they prayer he have undertake the war much more bitterly therefore do he complain of he be forsake xvii -- then at length liscus move by caesar 's speech disclose what he have hitherto keep secret : -- that there be some whose influence with the people be very great who though private man have more power than the magistrate themselves : that these by seditious and violent language be deter the populace from contribute the corn which they ought to supply -lrb- by tell they -rrb- that if they can not any longer retain the supremacy of gaul it be better to submit to the government of gaul than of roman nor ought they to doubt that if the roman should overpower the helvetius they would wrest they freedom from the aeduus together with the remainder of gaul by these very man -lrb- say he -rrb- be we plan and whatever be do in the camp disclose to the enemy that they could not be restrain by _ he _ : nay more he be well aware that though compel by necessity he have disclose the matter to caesar at how great a risk he have do it and for that reason he have be silent as long as he could xviii -- caesar perceive that by this speech of liscus dumnorix the brother of divitiacus be indicate but as he be unwilling that these matter should be discuss while so many be present he speedily dismiss the council but detain liscus : he inquire from he when alone about those thing which he have say in the meeting he -lrb- liscus -rrb- speak more unreservedly and boldly he -lrb- caesar -rrb- make inquiry on the same point privately of other and discover that it be all true that dumnorix be the person a man of the highest daring in great favor with the people on account of he liberality a man eager for a revolution : that for a great many year he have be in the habit of contracting for the custom and all the other tax of the aeduus at a small cost because when _ he _ bid no one dare to bid against he by these means he have both increase he own private property and amass great means for give largess that he maintain constantly at he own expense and keep about he own person a great number of cavalry and that not only at home but even among the neighbouring state he have great influence and for the sake of strengthen this influence have give he mother in marriage among the biturige to a man the most noble and most influential there that he have himself take a wife from among the helvetius and have give he sister by the mother 's side and he female relation in marriage into other state that he favor and wish well to the helvetius on account of this connection and that he hate caesar and the roman on he own account because by they arrival he power be weaken and he brother divitiacus restore to he former position of influence and dignity : that if anything should happen to the roman he entertain the highest hope of gain the sovereignty by means of the helvetius but that under the government of the roman people he despair not only of royalty but even of that influence which he already have caesar discover too on inquire into the unsuccessful cavalry engagement which have take place a few day before that the commencement of that flight have be make by dumnorix and he cavalry -lrb- for dumnorix be in command of the cavalry which the aeduus have send for aid to caesar -rrb- that by they flight the rest of the cavalry be dismayed xix -- after learn these circumstance since to these suspicion the most unequivocal fact be add viz that he have lead the helvetius through the territory of the sequanus that he have provide that hostage should be mutually give that he have do all these thing not only without any order of he -lrb- caesar 's -rrb- and of he own state 's but even without they -lrb- the aeduus -rrb- know anything of it themselves that he -lrb- dumnorix -rrb- be reprimand by the -lrb- chief -rrb- magistrate of the aeduus he -lrb- caesar -rrb- consider that there be sufficient reason why he should either punish he himself or order the state to do so one thing -lrb- however -rrb- stand in the way of all this -- that he have learn by experience he brother divitiacus 's very high regard for the roman people he great affection towards he he distinguished faithfulness justice and moderation for he be afraid lest by the punishment of this man he should hurt the feeling of divitiacus therefore before he attempt anything he order divitiacus to be summon to he and when the ordinary interpreter have be withdraw converse with he through caius valerius procillus chief of the province of gaul a intimate friend of he in whom he repose the highest confidence in everything at the same time he remind he of what be say about dumnorix in the council of the gaul when he himself be present and show what each have say of he privately in he -lrb- caesar 's -rrb- own presence he beg and exhort he that without offence to he feeling he may either himself pass judgment on he -lrb- dumnorix -rrb- after try the case or else order the -lrb- aeduan -rrb- state to do so xx-divitiacus embrace caesar begin to implore he with many tear that he would not pass any very severe sentence upon he brother say that he know that those -lrb- charge -rrb- be true and that nobody suffer more pain on that account than he himself do for when he himself could effect a very great deal by he influence at home and in the rest of gaul and he -lrb- dumnorix -rrb- very little on account of he youth the latter have become powerful through he means which power and strength he use not only to the lessen of he -lrb- divitiacus -rrb- popularity but almost to he ruin that he however be influence both by fraternal affection and by public opinion but if anything very severe from caesar should befall he -lrb- dumnorix -rrb- no one would think that it have be do without he consent since he himself hold such a place in caesar 's friendship from which circumstance it would arise that the affection of the whole of gaul would be estrange from he as he be with tear beg these thing of caesar in many word caesar take he right hand and comforting he beg he to make a end of entreat and assure he that he regard for he be so great that he forgive both the injury of the republic and he private wrong at he desire and prayer he summons dumnorix to he he bring in he brother he point out what he censure in he he lay before he what he of himself perceive and what the state complain of he warn he for the future to avoid all grounds of suspicion he say that he pardon the past for the sake of he brother divitiacus he set spy over dumnorix that he may be able to know what he do and with whom he communicate xxi -- be on the same day inform by he scout that the enemy have encamp at the foot of a mountain eight mile from he own camp he send person to ascertain what the nature of the mountain be and of what kind the ascent on every side word be bring back that it be easy during the third watch he order titus labienus he lieutenant with praetorian power to ascend to the highest ridge of the mountain with two legion and with those as guide who have examine the road he explain what he plan be he himself during the fourth watch hasten to they by the same route by which the enemy have go and send on all the cavalry before he publius considius who be repute to be very experience in military affair and have be in the army of lucius sullum and afterwards in that of marcus crassus be send forward with the scout xxii -- at day-break when the summit of the mountain be in the possession of titus labienus and he himself be not further off than a mile and half from the enemy 's camp nor as he afterwards ascertain from the captive have either he arrival or that of labienus be discover considius with he horse at full gallop come up to he -- say that the mountain which he -lrb- caesar -rrb- wish should be seize by labienus be in possession of the enemy that he have discover this by the gallic arm and ensign caesar lead off he force to the next hill : -lrb- and -rrb- draw they up in battle-order labienus as he have be order by caesar not to come to a engagement unless -lrb- caesar 's -rrb- own force be see near the enemy 's camp that the attack upon the enemy might be make on every side at the same time be after have take possession of the mountain wait for we man and refrain from battle when at length the day be far advance caesar learn through spy that the mountain be in possession of he own man and that the helvetius have move they camp and that considius strike with fear have report to he as see that which he have not see on that day he follow the enemy at he usual distance and pitch he camp three mile from theirs xxiii -- the next day -lrb- as there remain in all only two day ' space -lrb- to the time -rrb- when he must serve out the corn to he army and as he be not more than eighteen mile from bibracte by far the largest and best-stored town of the aeduus -rrb- he think that he ought to provide for a supply of corn and divert he march from the helvetius and advance rapidly to bibracte this circumstance be report to the enemy by some deserter from lucius aemilius a captain of the gallic horse the helvetius either because they think that the roman strike with terror be retreat from they the more so as the day before though they have seize on the higher grounds they have not join battle or because they flatter themselves that they might be cut off from the provision alter they plan and change they route begin to pursue and to annoy we man in the rear xxiv -- caesar when he observe this draw off he force to the next hill and send the cavalry to sustain the attack of the enemy he himself meanwhile draw up on the middle of the hill a triple line of he four veteran legion in such a manner that he place above he on the very summit the two legion which he have lately levy in hither gaul and all the auxiliary and he order that the whole mountain should be cover with man and that meanwhile the baggage should be bring together into one place and the position be protect by those who be post in the upper line the helvetii have follow with all they waggon collect they baggage into one place : they themselves after have repulse we cavalry and form a phalanx advance up to we front line in very close order xxv -- caesar have remove out of sight first he own horse then those of all that he might make the danger of all equal and do away with the hope of flight after encourage he man join battle he soldier hurl they javelin from the higher ground easily break the enemy 's phalanx that be disperse they make a charge on they with draw sword it be a great hindrance to the gaul in fight that when several of they buckler have be by one stroke of the -lrb- roman -rrb- javelin pierce through and pin fast together as the point of the iron have bent itself they could neither pluck it out nor with they left hand entangled fight with sufficient ease so that many after have long toss they arm about choose rather to cast away the buckler from they hand and to fight with they person unprotected at length wear out with wound they begin to give way and as there be in the neighborhood a mountain about a mile off to betake themselves thither when the mountain have be gain and we man be advance up the boius and tulingus who with about 15000 man close the enemy 's line of march and serve as a guard to they rear have assail we man on the expose flank as they advance -lrb- prepared -rrb- to surround they upon see which the helvetius who have betake themselves to the mountain begin to press on again and renew the battle the roman have face about advance to the attack in two division the first and second line to withstand those who have be defeat and drive off the field the third to receive those who be just arrive xxvi -- thus be the contest long and vigorously carry on with doubtful success when they could no longer withstand the attack of we man the one division as they have begin to do betake themselves to the mountain the other repair to they baggage and waggon for during the whole of this battle although the fight last from the seventh hour -lrb- _ ie _ 12 -lrb- noon -rrb- -- 1 pm -rrb- to eventide no one could see a enemy with he back turn the fight be carry on also at the baggage till late in the night for they have set waggon in the way as a rampart and from the higher ground keep throw weapon upon we man as they come on and some from between the waggon and the wheel keep dart they lance and javelin from beneath and wound we man after the fight have last some time we man gain possession of they baggage and camp there the daughter and one of the son of orgetorix be take after that battle about 130000 man -lrb- of the enemy -rrb- remain alive who march incessantly during the whole of that night and after a march discontinue for no part of the night arrive in the territory of the lingone on the fourth day whilst we man have stop for three day both on account of the wound of the soldier and the burial of the slay have not be able to follow they caesar send letter and messenger to the lingone -lrb- with order -rrb- that they should not assist they with corn or with anything else for that if they should assist they he would regard they in the same light as the helvetius after the three day ' interval he begin to follow they himself with all he force xxvii -- the helvetii compel by the want of everything send ambassador to he about a surrender when these have meet he in the way and have throw themselves at he foot and speak in suppliant tone have with tear sue for peace and -lrb- have be violently struggle with one another for the superiority for many year it come to pass that the german be call in for hire by the arvernus and the sequanus that about 15000 of they -lrb- _ ie _ of the german -rrb- have at first cross the rhine : but after that these wild and savage man have become enamoured of the land and the refinement and the abundance of the gaul more be bring over that -rrb- that he wish to treat with he on state-business and matter of the highest importance to both of they to this embassy ariovistus reply that if he himself have have need of anything from caesar he successive day from that day caesar draw out he force before the camp and put they in battle order that if ariovistus should be willing to engage in battle a opportunity might not be want to he active and courageous foot one of whom each of the horse select out of the whole army for he own protection by these -lrb- foot -rrb- they be constantly accompany in they engagement to these the horse retire these on any emergency rush forward if any one upon receive a very severe wound have fall from he horse they stand around he : if it be necessary to advance farther : than usual or to retreat more rapidly so great from practice be they swiftness that support by the mane of the horse they could keep pace with they speed xlix -- perceive that ariovistus keep himself in camp caesar that he might not any longer be cut off from provision choose a convenient position for a camp beyond that place in which the german have encamp at about 600 pace from they and have draw up he army in three line march to that place he order the first and second line to be under arm the third to fortify the camp this place be distant from the enemy about 600 pace as have be state thither ariovistus send light troops about 16000 man in number with all he cavalry which force be to intimidate we man and hinder they in they fortification caesar nevertheless as he have before arrange order two line to drive off the enemy : the third to execute the work the camp be fortify he leave there two legion and a portion of the auxiliary and lead back the other four legion into the larger camp l -- the next day accord to he custom caesar lead out he force from both camp and have advance a little from the larger one draw up he line of battle and give the enemy a opportunity of fight when he find that they do not even then come out -lrb- from they entrenchment -rrb- he lead back he army into camp about noon then at last ariovistus send part of he force to attack the lesser camp the battle be vigorously maintain on both side till the evening at sunset after many wound have be inflict and receive ariovistus lead back he force into camp when caesar inquire of he prisoner wherefore ariovistus do not come to a engagement he discover this to be the reason -- that among the german it be the custom for they matron to pronounce from lot and divination whether it be expedient that the battle should be engage in or not that they have say that it be not the will of heaven that the german should conquer if they engage in battle before the new moon li -- the day follow caesar leave what seem sufficient as a guard for both camp -lrb- and then -rrb- draw up all the auxiliary in sight of the enemy before the lesser camp because he be not very powerful in the number of legionary soldier consider the number of the enemy that -lrb- thereby -rrb- he might make use of he auxiliary for appearance he himself have draw up he army in three line advance to the camp of the enemy then at last of necessity the german draw they force out of camp and dispose they canton by canton at equal distance the harude marcomannus triboccus vangione nemete sedusius suevus and surround they whole army with they chariot and waggon that no hope might be leave in flight on these they place they woman who with dishevelled hair and in tear entreat the soldier as they go forward to battle not to deliver they into slavery to the roman lii -- caesar appoint over each legion a lieutenant and a questor that every one might have they as witness of he valor he himself begin the battle at the head of the right wing because he have observe that part of the enemy to be the least strong accordingly we man upon the signal be give vigorously make a attack upon the enemy and the enemy so suddenly and rapidly rush forward that there be no time for cast the javelin at they throw aside -lrb- therefore -rrb- they javelin they fight with sword hand to hand but the german accord to they custom rapidly form a phalanx sustain the attack of we sword there be find very many of we soldier who leap upon the phalanx and with they hand tear away the shield and wound the enemy from above although the army of the enemy be route on the left wing and put to flight they -lrb- still -rrb- press heavily on we man from the right wing by the great number of they troops on observe which p crassus a young man who command the cavalry -- as he be more disengaged than those who be employ in the fight -- send the third line as a relief to we man who be in distress liii -- thereupon the engagement be renew and all the enemy turn they back nor do they cease to flee until they arrive at the river rhine about fifty mile from that place there some few either rely on they strength endeavor to swim over or find boat procure they safety among the latter be ariovistus who meet with a small vessel tie to the bank escape in it : we horse pursued and slew all the rest of they ariovistus have two wife one a suevan by nation whom he have bring with he from home the other a norican the sister of king vocion whom he have marry in gaul she have be send -lrb- thither for that purpose -rrb- by she brother both perish in that flight of they two daughter one be slay the other capture c valerius procillus as he be be drag by he guard in the flight bind with a triple chain fall into the hand of caesar himself as he be pursue the enemy with he cavalry this circumstance indeed afford caesar no less pleasure than the victory itself because he see a man of the first rank in the province of gaul he intimate acquaintance and friend rescue from the hand of the enemy and restore to he and that fortune have not diminish aught of the joy and exultation -lrb- of that day -rrb- by he destruction he -lrb- procillus -rrb- say that in he own presence the lot have be thrice consult respect he whether he should immediately be put to death by fire or be reserve for another time : that by the favor of the lot he be uninjured m mettius also be find and bring back to he -lrb- caesar -rrb- liv -- this battle have be report beyond the rhine the suevus who have come to the bank of that river begin to return home when the ubius who dwell nearest to the rhine pursue they while much alarmed slew a great number of they caesar have conclude two very important war in one campaign conduct he army into winter quarter among the sequanus a little earlier than the season of the year require he appoint labienus over the winter quarter and set out in person for hither gaul to hold the assizes book ii i -- while caesar be in winter quarter in hither gaul as we have show above frequent report be bring to he and he be also inform by letter from labienus that all the belga who we have say be a third part of gaul be enter into a confederacy against the roman people and give hostage to one another that the reason of the confederacy be these -- first because they fear that after all -lrb- celtic -rrb- gaul be subdue we army would be lead against they secondly because they be instigate by several of the gaul some of whom as -lrb- on the one hand -rrb- they have be unwilling that the german should remain any longer in gaul so -lrb- on the other -rrb- they be dissatisfy that the army of the roman people should pass the winter in it and settle there and other of they from a natural instability and fickleness of disposition be anxious for a revolution -lrb- the belga be instigate -rrb- by several also because the government in gaul be generally seize upon by the more powerful person and by those who have the means of hire troops and they could less easily effect this object under we dominion ii -- alarm by these tiding and letter caesar levy two new legion in hither gaul and at the beginning of summer send q pedius he lieutenant to conduct they further into gaul he himself as soon as there begin to be plenty of forage come to the army he give a commission to the senone and the other gaul who be neighbor of the belgae to learn what be go on amongst they -lrb- _ ie _ the belga -rrb- and inform he of these matter these all uniformly report that troops be be raise and that a army be be collect in one place then indeed he think that he ought not to hesitate about proceed towards they and have provide supplies move he camp and in about fifteen day arrive at the territory of the belgae iii -- as he arrive there unexpectedly and sooner than any one anticipate the remus who be the nearest of the belgae to -lrb- celtic -rrb- gaul send to he iccius and antebrogius -lrb- two of -rrb- the other thing that all the rest of the belgae be in arm and that the german who dwell on this side the rhine have join themselves to they and that so great be the infatuation of they all that they could not restrain even the suessione they own brethren and kinsman who enjoy the same rights and the same law and who have one government and one magistracy -lrb- in common -rrb- with themselves from unite with they iv -- when caesar inquire of they what state be in arm how powerful they be and what they could do in war he receive the follow information : that the greater part of the belgae be spring from the german and that have cross the rhine at a early period they have settle there on account of the fertility of the country and have drive out the gaul who inhabit those region and that they be the only people who in the memory of we father when all gaul be overrun have prevent the teutone and the cimbrus from enter they territory the effect of which be that from the recollection of those event they assume to themselves great authority and haughtiness in military matter the remus say that they have know accurately everything respect they number because be unite to they by neighborhood and by alliance they have learn what number each state have in the general council of the belgae promise for that war that the bellovacus be the most powerful amongst they in valor influence and number of man that these could muster 100000 armed man -lrb- and have -rrb- promise 60000 pick man out of that number and demand for themselves the command of the whole war that the suessione be they nearest neighbor and possess a very extensive and fertile country that among they even in we own memory divitiacus the most powerful man of all gaul have be king who have hold the government of a great part of these region as well as of britain that they king at present be galba that the direction of the whole war be confer by the consent of all upon he on account of he integrity and prudence whilst the legion in the different place be withstand the enemy some in one quarter some in another and the view be obstruct by the very thick hedge intervene as we have before remark neither could proper reserve be post nor could the necessary measure be take in each part nor could all the command be issue by one person therefore in such a unfavourable state of affair various event of fortune to the right wing where he perceive that he man be hard press and that in consequence of the standard of the twelfth legion be collect together in one place the crowded soldier be a hindrance to themselves in the fight that all the centurion of the fourth cohort be slay and the standard-bearer kill the standard itself lose almost all the centurion of the other cohort either wound or slay and among they the chief centurion of the legion p sextius baculus a very valiant man who be so exhaust by many and severe wound that he be already unable to support himself he likewise perceive that the rest be slacken they effort and that some desert by those in the rear be retire from the battle and avoid the weapon that the enemy -lrb- on the other hand -rrb- though advance from the lower ground be not relax in front and be -lrb- at the same time -rrb- press hard on both flank he also perceive that the affair be at a crisis and that there be not any reserve which could be bring up have therefore snatch a shield from one of the soldier in the rear -lrb- for he himself have come without a shield -rrb- he advance to the front of the line and address the centurion by name and encourage the rest of the soldier he order they to carry forward the standard and extend the company that they might the more easily use they sword on he arrival as hope be bring to the soldier and they courage restore whilst every one for he own part in the sight of he general desire to exert he utmost energy the impetuosity of the enemy be a little check xxvi -- caesar when he perceive that the seventh legion which stand close by he be also hard press by the enemy direct the tribune of the soldier to effect a junction of the legion gradually and make they charge upon the enemy with a double front which have be do since they bring assistance the one to the other nor fear lest they rear should be surround by the enemy they begin to stand they ground more boldly and to fight more courageously in though unarmed see the enemy completely dismayed attack -lrb- they though -rrb- arm the horseman too that they might by they valor blot out the disgrace of they flight thrust themselves before the fight from they body when these be overthrow and they corpse heap up together those who survive cast they weapon against we man -lrb- thence -rrb- as from a mound and return we dart which have fall dependant from offer injury or outrage -lrb- to they -rrb- xxix -- when the aduatucus of whom we have write above be come with all they force to the assistance of the nervius upon this battle be report to they they return home after they be on the march desert all they town and fort they convey together all they possession into one town eminently fortify by nature while this town have on all they have place stone of great weight and sharpened stake upon the wall they be descend from the cimbrus and teutone who when they be march into we province and italy have deposit on this side the river rhine such of they baggage-train as they could not drive or convey with they leave 6000 of they man as a guard and defense for they these have after the destruction of they countryman be harass settlement xxx -- and on the first arrival of we army they make frequent sally from the town and contend with we man in trifling skirmish : afterwards when hem in by a rampart of twelve foot -lrb- in height -rrb- and fifteen mile in circuit they keep themselves within the town when vineae have be bring up and a mound raise they observe that a tower also be be build at a distance they at first begin to mock the roman from they wall and to taunt they with the follow speech for what purpose be so vast a machine construct at so great a distance with what hand or with what strength do they especially -lrb- as they be -rrb- man of such very small stature -lrb- for we shortness of stature in comparison with the great size of they body be generally a subject of much contempt to the man of gaul -rrb- trust to place against they wall a tower of such great weight xxxi -- but when they see that it be be move and be approach they wall startle by the new and unaccustomed sight they send ambassador to caesar -lrb- to treat -rrb- about peace who speak in the follow manner : that they do not believe the roman wage war without divine aid since they be able to move forward machine of such a height with so great speed and thus fight from close quarter : that they resign themselves and all they possession to -lrb- caesar 's -rrb- disposal : that they beg and earnestly entreat one thing viz that if perchance agreeably to he clemency and humanity which they have hear of from other he should resolve that the aduatucus be to be spare he would not deprive they of they arm that all they neighbor be enemy to they and envy they courage from whom they could not defend themselves if they arm be deliver up : that it be better for they if they should be reduce to that state to suffer any fate from the roman people than to be tortured to death by those among whom they have be accustom to rule xxxii -- to these thing caesar reply that he in accordance with he custom rather than owe to they desert should spare the state if they should surrender themselves before the battering-ram should touch the wall but that there be no condition of surrender except upon they arm be deliver up that he should do to they that which he have do in the case of the nervius and would command they neighbor not to offer any injury to those who have surrender to the roman people the matter be report to they countryman they say that they would execute he command have cast a very large quantity of they arm from the wall into the trench which be before the town so that the heap of arm almost equal towards evening caesar order the gate to be shut and the soldier to go out of the town lest the townspeople should receive any injury from they by night they -lrb- the aduatucus -rrb- by a design the man have be slay the rest be force back into the town the day after caesar after break open the gate which there be no one then to defend and send in we soldier sell the whole spoil of that town servius galba with the twelfth legion and part of the cavalry against the nantuate the veragrus and sedunus who extend from the territory of the allobroge and the lake of geneva and the river rhone to ditch ii -- when several day have elapse in winter quarter and he have order corn to be bring in he be suddenly inform by he scout that all the people have go off in the night from that part of the have be station and recover v -- when they have now be fight for more than six hour without cessation and not only strength but even weapon be fail we man and the enemy be press on more rigorously and the army the venetus and the other state also be inform of caesar 's arrival when they reflect how great a crime they have commit in that the ambassador -lrb- a character which have amongst all nation ever be sacred and inviolable -rrb- have by they be detain and throw into prison resolve to prepare for a war in proportion to the greatness of they danger and especially to provide those thing which appertain to the service of a navy with the greater confidence inasmuch as they greatly rely on the nature of they situation they know that the pass by land be cut off by estuary that the approach by sea be most difficult by reason of we ignorance of the locality -lrb- and -rrb- the small number of the harbor and they trust that we army would not be able to stay very long among they on account of the insufficiency of corn and again even if all these thing should turn out contrary to they expectation yet they be very powerful in they navy they well understand that the roman neither have any number of ship nor be acquaint with the shallows the harbor or the island of those part where they would have to carry on the war and that navigation be very different in a narrow sea from what it be in the vast and open ocean have come to this resolution they fortify they town convey corn into they from the country part bring together as many ship as possible to venetium where it appear caesar would at first carry on the war they unite to themselves as ally for that war the osismius the lexovius the nannete the ambiliatus the morinus the diablinte and the menapius and send for auxiliary from britain which be situate over against those region x -- there be these difficulty which we have mention above in carry on the war but many thing nevertheless urge caesar to that war the open insult offer to the state in the detention of the roman knight the rebellion raise after surrender the revolt after hostage be give the confederacy of so many state but principally lest if -lrb- the conduct of -rrb- this part be overlook the other nation should think that the same thing be permit they wherefore since he reflect that almost all the gaul be fond of revolution and easily and quickly excite to war that all man likewise by nature love liberty and hate the condition of slavery he think he ought to divide and more widely distribute he army before more state should join the confederation xi -- he therefore send t labienus he lieutenant with the cavalry to the trevirus who be nearest to the river rhine he charge he to visit the remus and the other belgian and to keep they in they allegiance and repel the german -lrb- who be say to have be summon by the belgae to they aid -rrb- if they attempt to cross the river by force in they ship he order p crassus to proceed into hasten thither with the land force xii -- the site of they town be generally such that be place on extreme point -lrb- of land -rrb- and on promontory they neither have a approach by land when the tide have rush in from the main ocean which always happen twice in the space of twelve hour nor by ship because upon the tide ebb again the ship be likely to be dash upon the shoal thus by either circumstance be the storm of they town render difficult and if at any time perchance the venetus overpower by the greatness of we work -lrb- the sea have be exclude by a mound and large dam and the latter be make almost equal in height to the wall of the town -rrb- have begin to despair of they fortune bring up a large number of ship of which they have a very great quantity they carry off all they property and betook themselves to the nearest town there they again defend themselves by the same advantage of situation they do this the more easily during a great part of the summer because we ship be keep back by storm and the difficulty of sailing be very great in that vast and open sea with its strong tide and its harbor far apart and exceedingly few in number xiii -- for they ship be build and equip after this manner the keel be somewhat flatter than those of we ship whereby they could more easily encounter the shallows and the ebbing of the tide : the prow be raise very high and in like manner the stern be adapt to the force of the wave and storm -lrb- which they be form to sustain -rrb- the ship be build wholly of oak and design to endure any force and violence whatever the bench which be make of plank a foot in breadth be fasten by iron spike of the thickness of a man 's thumb the anchor be secure fast by iron chain instead of cable and for sail they use skin and thin dress leather these -lrb- be use -rrb- either through they want of canvas and they ignorance of its application of for this reason which be more probable that they think that such storm of the ocean and such violent gale of wind could not be resist by sail nor ship of such great burden be conveniently enough manage by they the encounter of we fleet with these ship be of such a nature that we fleet excel in speed alone and the ply of the oar other thing consider the nature of the place -lrb- and -rrb- the violence of the storm be more suitable and better adapt on they side for neither could we ship injure theirs with they beak -lrb- so great be they strength -rrb- nor on account of they height be a weapon easily cast up to they and for the same reason they be less readily lock in by rock to this be add that whenever a storm begin to rage and they run before the wind they both could weather the storm more easily and heave to securely in the shallows and when leave by the tide fear nothing from rock and shelf : the risk of all which thing be much to be dread by we ship xiv -- caesar after take many of they town perceive that so much labor be spend in vain and that the flight of the enemy could not be prevent on the capture of they town and that injury could not be do they he determine to wait for he fleet as soon as it come up and be first see by the enemy about 220 of they ship fully equip and appoint with every kind of -lrb- naval -rrb- implement sail forth from the harbor and draw up opposite to ours nor do it appear clear to brutus who command the fleet or to the tribune of the soldier and the centurion to whom the several ship be assign what to do or what system of tactic to adopt for they know that damage could not be do by they beak and that although turret be build -lrb- on they deck -rrb- yet the height of the stem of the barbarian ship exceed these so that weapon could not be cast up from -lrb- we -rrb- lower position with sufficient effect and those cast by the gaul fall the more forcibly upon we one thing provide by we man be of great service -lrb- viz -rrb- sharp hook insert into and fasten upon pole of a form not unlike the hook use in attack town wall when the rope which fasten the sail-yard to the mast be catch by they and pull and we vessel vigorously impel with the oar they -lrb- the rope -rrb- be sever and when they be cut away the yard necessarily fall down so that as all the hope of the gallic vessel depend on they sail and rig upon these be cut away the entire management of the ship be take from they at the same time the rest of the contest depend on courage in which we man decidedly have the advantage and the more so because the whole action be carry on in the sight of caesar and the entire army so that no act a little more valiant than ordinary could pass unobserved for all the hill and higher grounds from which there be a near prospect of the sea be occupy by we army xv -- the sail-yard -lrb- of the enemy -rrb- as we have say be bring down although two and -lrb- in some case -rrb- three ship -lrb- of theirs -rrb- surround each one -lrb- of ours -rrb- the soldier strive with the greatest these thing be go on amongst the veneti q titurius sabinus with those troops which he have receive from caesar arrive in the territory of the unellus over these people viridovix rule and hold the somewhat taunt by the speech of we soldier and furnish so great a suspicion of he cowardice that the enemy presume to approach even to the very rampart of we camp he adopt this conduct for the follow reason : because he do not think that a lieutenant ought to engage in battle with so great a force especially while he who hold the chief command be absent except on advantageous ground or some favourable circumstance present itself xviii -- after have establish this suspicion of he cowardice he select a certain suitable and crafty gaul who be one of those whom he have with he as auxiliary
